entry point for configuring the entire authentication stack\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/SaslChannelBuilder.java` — builds `KafkaChannel` instances with `SaslServerAuthenticator`; selects per-mechanism callback handlers (PLAIN, SCRAM, OAUTHBEARER, GSSAPI)\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/KafkaChannel.java` — wraps the transport layer and authenticator; drives the authentication state machine\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/NetworkReceive.java` — size-delimited byte reader; enforces `saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize` cap during auth\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerAuthenticator.java` — core server-side SASL state machine (`INITIAL_REQUEST` → `AUTHENTICATE` → `COMPLETE`); processes raw untrusted bytes via `handleKafkaRequest()` and `handleSaslToken()`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerCallbackHandler.java` — default callback handler for GSSAPI; unconditionally approves `AuthorizeCallback`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java` — extracts `KafkaPrincipal` from completed `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()`; applies Kerberos short-naming rules for GSSAPI\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/auth/KafkaPrincipal.java` — holds `principalType` + `name` used by ACL matching; mutable `tokenAuthenticated` flag\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainSaslServer.java` — PLAIN mechanism; parses NUL-delimited credential bytes; sets `authorizationId = username` from client\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` — PLAIN credential verifier; constructs JAAS key `user_<username>` from client-supplied name; uses constant-time comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramSaslServer.java` — SCRAM state machine (client-first → server-first → client-final); retrieves stored key from `ScramServerCallbackHandler`; verifies with HMAC constant-time comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramServerCallbackHandler.java` — SCRAM credential verifier; looks up credentials by client-supplied username from `CredentialCache`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramMessages.java` — SCRAM message parser using regex patterns; parses `saslName`, `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions` from untrusted bytes\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` — OAUTHBEARER mechanism; parses client initial response and delegates JWT validation to callback handler; extracts `principalName` from validated token\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.java` — parses raw OAUTHBEARER client message; extracts Bearer token string and extensions using regex\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — production OAUTHBEARER validator; verifies JWT signature against JWKS endpoint\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/unsecured/OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — **development-only** OAUTHBEARER validator that accepts unsigned JWTs; used as the default in `SaslChannelBuilder` when no custom handler is configured\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/authorizer/Authorizer.java` — ACL authorization interface; `authorize(requestContext, actions)` is called per Kafka request using the extracted `KafkaPrincipal`\n- `metadata/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/metadata/authorizer/StandardAuthorizerData.java` — KRaft ACL engine; matches `KafkaPrincipal` against ACL entries with wildcard and prefix support; hard-codes super-user bypass\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/JaasContext.java` — loads JAAS configuration entries; provides `configEntryOption()` used by PLAIN to resolve per-user passwords\n\n---\n\n## Entry Points\n\n1. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — accepts raw bytes from the network transport layer representing the entire SASL exchange; all untrusted client data flows through here\n2. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` — accepts raw request bytes; parses `ApiVersionsRequest` (client software name/version) and `SaslHandshakeRequest` (mechanism name selection) from untrusted client\n3. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` — accepts raw SASL token bytes (post-handshake); forwards to `SaslServer.evaluateResponse()` for mechanism-specific parsing\n4. `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts NUL-delimited byte payload from client containing `authzid`, `authcid`, and `passwd` as UTF-8 strings\n5. `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts SCRAM client-first and client-final message bytes including username, nonce, channel binding, extensions, and cryptographic proof\n6. `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts raw OAUTHBEARER client initial response bytes containing the JWT Bearer token string and arbitrary key-value extensions\n7. `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.java:<init>(byte[])` — parses the OAUTHBEARER message; regex-validates authzid, key-value pairs, and the `auth=Bearer <token>` value\n8. `ScramMessages.ClientFirstMessage.<init>(byte[])` — parses SCRAM client-first message bytes; extracts `saslName`, `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions` via regex\n9. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleApiVersionsRequest()` — registers client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings (from `ApiVersionsRequest`) into `ChannelMetadataRegistry`\n\n---\n\n## Data Flow\n\n### Flow 1: PLAIN Authentication — credential-bearing bytes to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (lines 262–274) — raw bytes read from `TransportLayer` into `netInBuffer`; `clientToken` byte array populated from socket\n2. **Dispatch**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 423) — `clientToken` passed as-is to `saslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)` (or after unwrapping from `SaslAuthenticateRequest.authBytes()` at line 462)\n3. **Parse**: `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (lines 85–86) — bytes decoded as UTF-8 string; `extractTokens()` splits on NUL bytes to produce `[authzid, username, password]`\n4. **Callback**: `PlainSaslServer.java` (lines 98–104) — `NameCallback` set with client-supplied `username`; `PlainAuthenticateCallback` set with client-supplied `password` chars; `callbackHandler.handle()` invoked\n5. **Credential Lookup**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java:authenticate()` (lines 65–68) — JAAS key constructed as `\"user_\" + username` (directly from client); `JaasContext.configEntryOption()` retrieves expected password; `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` compares passwords\n6. **Authorization ID Set**: `PlainSaslServer.java` (line 110) — `this.authorizationId = username` (client-supplied value, after authentication success)\n7. **Principal Extraction**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:principal()` (lines 310–314) — builds `SaslAuthenticationContext`; calls `principalBuilder.build(context)`\n8. **Sink**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:build()` (line 84) — `new KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, saslServer.getAuthorizationID())` — client-supplied username becomes the authoritative principal name used for all subsequent ACL checks\n\n### Flow 2: SCRAM Authentication — multi-round challenge to stored-key verification\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — raw bytes from socket, two rounds\n2. **Round 1 Parse**: `ScramMessages.ClientFirstMessage.<init>(byte[])` (lines 82–92) — regex parses client-first message; extracts `saslName` (username, URL-encoded), `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions`\n3. **Username Decode**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (lines 108–109) — `ScramFormatter.username(saslName)` decodes `=2C`/`=3D` escapes; `NameCallback` set with decoded username\n4. **Credential Lookup**: `ScramServerCallbackHandler.java:handle()` (lines 65–68) — `credentialCache.get(username)` looks up `ScramCredential` by client-supplied username; if absent, authentication fails with generic error\n5. **Server Challenge**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (lines 135–140) — server combines client nonce + server nonce, returns salt and iteration count; state advances to `RECEIVE_CLIENT_FINAL_MESSAGE`\n6. **Round 2 Parse**: `ScramMessages.ClientFinalMessage.<init>(byte[])` — parses client-final message containing channel-binding, full nonce, and HMAC proof\n7. **Nonce Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (line 150–151) — `clientFinalMessage.nonce()` must equal server-generated nonce; prevents replay\n8. **Proof Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java:verifyClientProof()` (lines 229–233) — `MessageDigest.isEqual(computedStoredKey, expectedStoredKey)` — time-constant comparison\n9. **Sink**: `ScramSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` returns `authorizationId` (client-supplied username, post-authentication) → `KafkaPrincipal`\n\n### Flow 3: OAUTHBEARER Authentication — JWT token to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 95) — raw bytes parsed by `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse`\n2. **Parse**: `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.<init>(byte[])` (lines 51–76) — regex extracts `authzid`, key-value extension pairs, and the `auth=Bearer <token>` string; `tokenValue` is the raw JWT string\n3. **Validation**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:process()` (lines 154–160) — `OAuthBearerValidatorCallback` constructed with client-supplied JWT string; `callbackHandler.handle()` invoked — validation quality depends entirely on which handler is configured\n4. **Default Handler Gap**: `SaslChannelBuilder.java` (line 331) — if no custom handler configured, `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is used, which accepts **unsigned** JWTs without signature verification\n5. **AuthzId Check**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` (line 171–173) — if client provides `authorizationId`, it must match `token.principalName()`; if empty, `token.principalName()` is used directly\n6. **Extensions**: `processExtensions()` validates extension key-value pairs; malformed extensions rejected\n7. **Sink**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 108) — returns `tokenForNegotiatedProperty.principalName()` from the JWT claims → `KafkaPrincipal`\n\n### Flow 4: GSSAPI Kerberos Authentication — GSS tokens to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — raw Kerberos AP-REQ/AP-REP tokens from client\n2. **GSSAPI Processing**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:createSaslKerberosServer()` — `Sasl.createSaslServer()` creates JVM GSSAPI `SaslServer`; GSS token evaluation performed by the JVM's Kerberos implementation\n3. **Callback**: `SaslServerCallbackHandler.java:handleAuthorizeCallback()` (lines 67–73) — **unconditionally** sets `ac.setAuthorized(true)` and `ac.setAuthorizedID(authenticationID)`; no additional authorization checks performed here\n4. **Short-naming**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:applyKerberosShortNamer()` — applies configured `sasl.kerberos.principal.to.local` rules to transform `user@REALM` to short name\n5. **Sink**: `new KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, shortName)` → ACL matching uses the short name\n\n### Flow 5: Principal to ACL Authorization\n\n1. **Post-authentication**: `KafkaChannel` marks channel as authenticated; `KafkaPrincipal` stored\n2. **Per-request authorization**: Broker request handler calls `Authorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)` per incoming Kafka API request\n3. **ACL lookup**: `StandardAuthorizerData` matches `requestContext.principal()` (the `KafkaPrincipal`) against stored ACL bindings by `principalType:name`, with wildcard `User:*` support\n4. **Super-user bypass**: If `principal.getName()` appears in the configured `super.users` list, all ACL checks are bypassed\n5. **Result**: `ALLOWED` or `DENIED` returned per action; denied requests receive error responses\n\n---\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n```\n[TCP Socket]\n    ↓ raw bytes\nNetworkReceive (size-prefixed, max bounded by saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize)\n    ↓\nSaslServerAuthenticator.authenticate()  [SaslState machine]\n    ↓ INITIAL_REQUEST\nhandleKafkaRequest()\n    ↓ ApiVersionsRequest\n        → clientSoftwareName/Version → ChannelMetadataRegistry  [log injection risk]\n    ↓ SaslHandshakeRequest\n        → clientMechanism validated against enabledMechanisms\n        → createSaslServer(mechanism)\n            ├─ PLAIN  → PlainSaslServer + PlainServerCallbackHandler\n            ├─ SCRAM  → ScramSaslServer + ScramServerCallbackHandler → CredentialCache\n            ├─ OAUTHBEARER → OAuthBearerSaslServer + {OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler | OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler}\n            └─ GSSAPI → JVM GSSAPI SaslServer + SaslServerCallbackHandler\n    ↓ AUTHENTICATE\nhandleSaslToken(clientToken)\n    → SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)\n        [per-mechanism parsing, validation, credential lookup]\n    → saslServer.isComplete() == true\n    ↓ COMPLETE\nSaslServerAuthenticator.principal()\n    → SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()  [client-controlled value, authenticated]\n    → DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()\n    → KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, authorizationId)\n        ↓\nAuthorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)\n    → StandardAuthorizerData ACL matching on KafkaPrincipal\n    → AuthorizationResult.ALLOWED / DENIED\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### 1. Insecure Default OAUTHBEARER Validator (Authentication Bypass)\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Authentication bypass / missing cryptographic verification\n\n**Description**: In `SaslChannelBuilder.createServerCallbackHandlers()` (line 331), when the OAUTHBEARER mechanism is enabled without an explicit `sasl.server.callback.handler.class`, the default handler assigned is `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`. This handler is documented as \"unsecured\" and intended for development/testing only — it accepts self-signed JWTs without verifying the cryptographic signature or checking the issuer/audience claims.\n\n**Impact**: Any attacker can craft a JWT with an arbitrary `sub` (principal name) claim, sign it with any key (or not at all), and authenticate as any user — including super-users. This enables complete ACL bypass if super-users are OAUTHBEARER-authenticated, or impersonation of legitimate users.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The class is named \"Unsecured\" and documentation warns against production use. Operators must explicitly configure `OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler`.\n\n**Gap**: There is no runtime check preventing this insecure handler from being used in production deployments. No warning log is emitted at startup when OAUTHBEARER is enabled with the unsecured handler.\n\n**Attack scenario**:\n1. Broker has `OAUTHBEARER` in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms` with no custom handler configured\n2. Attacker crafts JWT: `{\"alg\":\"none\"}.{\"sub\":\"admin\"}.{}`\n3. Attacker authenticates as `User:admin`; if `super.users=User:admin` is configured, full broker access is granted\n\n**Remediation**: Emit a warning (or fail-fast) at broker startup when OAUTHBEARER is enabled and `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is active. Consider removing it from the default selection path entirely.\n\n---\n\n### 2. Client-Controlled Principal Name (Trust Boundary Issue)\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Improper input validation / trust boundary violation\n\n**Description**: Across all non-GSSAPI mechanisms, the `authorizationId` that becomes the `KafkaPrincipal` name is derived entirely from client-supplied data:\n- **PLAIN**: `authorizationId = username` from the NUL-delimited payload (`PlainSaslServer.java:110`)\n- **SCRAM**: `authorizationId = username` decoded from `saslName` in the client-first message (`ScramSaslServer.java:123`)\n- **OAUTHBEARER**: `authorizationId = token.principalName()` from the JWT's `sub` claim (which is client-supplied, though validated if signature verification is correctly configured)\n\n**Impact**: While credential verification does gate access, the exact string used for ACL matching is what the client asserts — subject only to the credential existing in the store. A user who knows a valid credential can set their principal name to any case variant, Unicode normalization form, or special-character form that might match a different ACL entry or super-user listing. String comparison in ACL matching is exact (Java `String.equals()`), so this is mitigated by default, but custom `KafkaPrincipalBuilder` implementations could mishandle these inputs.\n\n**PLAIN-specific risk**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.authenticate()` constructs the JAAS key as `JAAS_USER_PREFIX + username` = `\"user_\" + username`. The username comes directly from the client. A username containing newlines, equals signs, or other JAAS-special characters (e.g., `alice\\nuser_bob=secret`) could potentially interfere with JAAS config parsing in unusual implementations, though the standard `AppConfigurationEntry` map lookup mitigates most cases.\n\n**Existing mitigations**: Credential verification ensures the client must possess the correct credential for the claimed identity. `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` prevents timing-based username enumeration for PLAIN.\n\n**Gap**: There is no normalization or sanitization of usernames before they are stored as the principal name. Usernames with special characters that are valid from the credential store's perspective could create unexpected ACL-matching behavior.\n\n---\n\n### 3. No Transport-Level Enforcement for PLAIN Mechanism\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Cleartext credential transmission\n\n**Description**: `PLAIN` mechanism transmits credentials (username + password) as unencrypted bytes within the SASL exchange. There is no code-level enforcement preventing `PLAIN` from being used over a `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listener (unencrypted TCP). The broker configuration allows combining `SASL_PLAINTEXT` protocol with `PLAIN` mechanism, resulting in credentials transmitted in cleartext over the network.\n\n`PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()` operates identically regardless of whether the underlying transport is TLS-protected (`SASL_SSL`) or plaintext (`SASL_PLAINTEXT`).\n\n**Impact**: Network-level passive eavesdropping yields plaintext credentials, enabling account takeover.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: Documentation recommends `SASL_SSL` for PLAIN. The `SslClientAuth` configuration is set to `NONE` for SASL_SSL listeners (line 153 in ChannelBuilders.java) since SASL takes over authentication.\n\n**Gap**: No runtime warning is emitted when `PLAIN` is configured on `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners; no enforced policy prevents this combination.\n\n---\n\n### 4. GSSAPI: Unconditional Authorization in SaslServerCallbackHandler\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Missing authorization check\n\n**Description**: `SaslServerCallbackHandler.handleAuthorizeCallback()` (lines 67–73) unconditionally sets `ac.setAuthorized(true)` and sets `authorizedID = authenticationID` without any validation logic. This means any principal that successfully completes Kerberos mutual authentication is unconditionally authorized at the SASL layer — the `AuthorizeCallback` mechanism (intended to allow/deny specific authorization IDs) provides no additional protection.\n\n**Impact**: There is no mechanism at the SASL layer to distinguish between Kerberos principals that should and should not be authorized to connect. All validated Kerberos principals pass the SASL authorization check, relying entirely on subsequent Kafka ACL checks.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: Kafka ACL checks downstream provide per-operation authorization. Kerberos mutual authentication ensures the identity is genuine.\n\n**Gap**: The SASL authorization callback is a hook designed to permit, for example, cross-realm or impersonation scenarios to be controlled. Its unconditional implementation removes this control point.\n\n---\n\n### 5. Log Injection via Client-Supplied Software Name and Version\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Log injection / information leakage\n\n**Description**: In `SaslServerAuthenticator.handleApiVersionsRequest()` (lines 581–582), the client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings from the `ApiVersionsRequest` are stored directly in `ChannelMetadataRegistry` without sanitization. These strings are subsequently logged and stored as `ClientInformation`. A malicious client can inject arbitrary strings — including newlines, terminal escape sequences, or structured log patterns — into broker log files.\n\n**Impact**: Log pollution, log injection attacks against log management systems, and potential exploitation of downstream log parsers.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The `ApiVersionsRequest` schema has string length limits (max 255 characters per field as per the Kafka protocol schema).\n\n**Gap**: Content is not sanitized for control characters or newlines before logging. Log4j/SLF4J patterns do not automatically escape newlines in logged strings.\n\n---\n\n### 6. Mechanism Downgrade — No Protocol-Level Downgrade Protection\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Protocol downgrade attack\n\n**Description**: The `SaslHandshakeRequest` allows the client to select any mechanism from the server's advertised list. The `handleHandshakeRequest()` method (line 550) accepts any `clientMechanism` that is in `enabledMechanisms`. If both `SCRAM-SHA-256` and `PLAIN` are enabled, a man-in-the-middle (operating at the TCP layer, below the Kafka protocol) could modify the handshake to force the client to use `PLAIN`, resulting in credential transmission in cleartext.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: With `SASL_SSL`, the TLS channel protects against MITM at the TCP layer, preventing handshake modification. With `SASL_PLAINTEXT`, no protection exists.\n\n**Gap**: There is no client-side or server-side enforcement of minimum mechanism security requirements, and enabling insecure mechanisms alongside secure ones creates a downgrade surface.\n\n---\n\n### 7. Re-authentication State Machine — Partial Protection\n\n**Description**: The re-authentication flow in `SaslServerAuthenticator.reauthenticate()` correctly calls `reauthInfo.ensurePrincipalUnchanged(principal())` to prevent principal swapping during re-authentication. However, the mechanism must also remain unchanged (`saslMechanismUnchanged()` check at line 532), which prevents mechanism downgrade during re-auth.\n\n**Gap**: The state machine transitions to `REAUTH_BAD_MECHANISM` before updating failure metrics, introducing a window where the failed re-authentication counter is not incremented immediately. This does not constitute a security vulnerability but reduces observability.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL authentication subsystem implements a well-structured state machine (`SaslServerAuthenticator`) that gates channel access through a sequence of `ApiVersionsRequest` → `SaslHandshakeRequest` → mechanism-specific token exchange before accepting any Kafka protocol requests. Principal identity flows from `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()` (set by each mechanism implementation from client-supplied, credential-verified data) through `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder` into `KafkaPrincipal`, which is then used for all downstream ACL checks by `Authorizer.authorize()`.\n\n**The most significant security finding** is that the OAUTHBEARER mechanism defaults to `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` when no custom handler is configured — an implementation that accepts unsigned JWTs without cryptographic verification. In a production environment where this handler is active, any attacker can authenticate as an arbitrary user (including super-users), achieving complete Kafka cluster compromise. This is an authentication bypass of the highest severity.\n\nSecondary findings include: PLAIN mechanism carrying credentials in cleartext over `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners; the GSSAPI `AuthorizeCallback` being unconditionally approved (eliminating a potential control point); client-supplied software name/version strings being logged without sanitization (log injection); and the absence of downgrade protection when multiple mechanisms of differing security strength are enabled simultaneously.\n\nMitigations that **do work correctly** include: constant-time password comparison in PLAIN (`Utils.isEqualConstantTime`); HMAC-based proof verification in SCRAM using `MessageDigest.isEqual` (time-constant); configurable receive size limits (`saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize`) preventing buffer exhaustion during authentication; principal-change enforcement during re-authentication; and nonce verification in SCRAM preventing replay attacks.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":2506,"cache_read_input_tokens":79706,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":2506,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":18,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"c5dac569-0767-4162-a7c8-c6cc5408615a","uuid":"daf7775d-ec65-40f8-a74c-c49775a225f0"}

entry point for configuring the entire authentication stack\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/SaslChannelBuilder.java` — builds `KafkaChannel` instances with `SaslServerAuthenticator`; selects per-mechanism callback handlers (PLAIN, SCRAM, OAUTHBEARER, GSSAPI)\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/KafkaChannel.java` — wraps the transport layer and authenticator; drives the authentication state machine\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/network/NetworkReceive.java` — size-delimited byte reader; enforces `saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize` cap during auth\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerAuthenticator.java` — core server-side SASL state machine (`INITIAL_REQUEST` → `AUTHENTICATE` → `COMPLETE`); processes raw untrusted bytes via `handleKafkaRequest()` and `handleSaslToken()`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/SaslServerCallbackHandler.java` — default callback handler for GSSAPI; unconditionally approves `AuthorizeCallback`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/authenticator/DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java` — extracts `KafkaPrincipal` from completed `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()`; applies Kerberos short-naming rules for GSSAPI\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/auth/KafkaPrincipal.java` — holds `principalType` + `name` used by ACL matching; mutable `tokenAuthenticated` flag\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainSaslServer.java` — PLAIN mechanism; parses NUL-delimited credential bytes; sets `authorizationId = username` from client\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/plain/internals/PlainServerCallbackHandler.java` — PLAIN credential verifier; constructs JAAS key `user_<username>` from client-supplied name; uses constant-time comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramSaslServer.java` — SCRAM state machine (client-first → server-first → client-final); retrieves stored key from `ScramServerCallbackHandler`; verifies with HMAC constant-time comparison\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramServerCallbackHandler.java` — SCRAM credential verifier; looks up credentials by client-supplied username from `CredentialCache`\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/scram/internals/ScramMessages.java` — SCRAM message parser using regex patterns; parses `saslName`, `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions` from untrusted bytes\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` — OAUTHBEARER mechanism; parses client initial response and delegates JWT validation to callback handler; extracts `principalName` from validated token\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.java` — parses raw OAUTHBEARER client message; extracts Bearer token string and extensions using regex\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — production OAUTHBEARER validator; verifies JWT signature against JWKS endpoint\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/oauthbearer/internals/unsecured/OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler.java` — **development-only** OAUTHBEARER validator that accepts unsigned JWTs; used as the default in `SaslChannelBuilder` when no custom handler is configured\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/server/authorizer/Authorizer.java` — ACL authorization interface; `authorize(requestContext, actions)` is called per Kafka request using the extracted `KafkaPrincipal`\n- `metadata/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/metadata/authorizer/StandardAuthorizerData.java` — KRaft ACL engine; matches `KafkaPrincipal` against ACL entries with wildcard and prefix support; hard-codes super-user bypass\n- `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/common/security/JaasContext.java` — loads JAAS configuration entries; provides `configEntryOption()` used by PLAIN to resolve per-user passwords\n\n---\n\n## Entry Points\n\n1. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — accepts raw bytes from the network transport layer representing the entire SASL exchange; all untrusted client data flows through here\n2. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleKafkaRequest()` — accepts raw request bytes; parses `ApiVersionsRequest` (client software name/version) and `SaslHandshakeRequest` (mechanism name selection) from untrusted client\n3. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` — accepts raw SASL token bytes (post-handshake); forwards to `SaslServer.evaluateResponse()` for mechanism-specific parsing\n4. `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts NUL-delimited byte payload from client containing `authzid`, `authcid`, and `passwd` as UTF-8 strings\n5. `ScramSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts SCRAM client-first and client-final message bytes including username, nonce, channel binding, extensions, and cryptographic proof\n6. `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` — accepts raw OAUTHBEARER client initial response bytes containing the JWT Bearer token string and arbitrary key-value extensions\n7. `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.java:<init>(byte[])` — parses the OAUTHBEARER message; regex-validates authzid, key-value pairs, and the `auth=Bearer <token>` value\n8. `ScramMessages.ClientFirstMessage.<init>(byte[])` — parses SCRAM client-first message bytes; extracts `saslName`, `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions` via regex\n9. `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleApiVersionsRequest()` — registers client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings (from `ApiVersionsRequest`) into `ChannelMetadataRegistry`\n\n---\n\n## Data Flow\n\n### Flow 1: PLAIN Authentication — credential-bearing bytes to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` (lines 262–274) — raw bytes read from `TransportLayer` into `netInBuffer`; `clientToken` byte array populated from socket\n2. **Dispatch**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:handleSaslToken()` (line 423) — `clientToken` passed as-is to `saslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)` (or after unwrapping from `SaslAuthenticateRequest.authBytes()` at line 462)\n3. **Parse**: `PlainSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (lines 85–86) — bytes decoded as UTF-8 string; `extractTokens()` splits on NUL bytes to produce `[authzid, username, password]`\n4. **Callback**: `PlainSaslServer.java` (lines 98–104) — `NameCallback` set with client-supplied `username`; `PlainAuthenticateCallback` set with client-supplied `password` chars; `callbackHandler.handle()` invoked\n5. **Credential Lookup**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.java:authenticate()` (lines 65–68) — JAAS key constructed as `\"user_\" + username` (directly from client); `JaasContext.configEntryOption()` retrieves expected password; `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` compares passwords\n6. **Authorization ID Set**: `PlainSaslServer.java` (line 110) — `this.authorizationId = username` (client-supplied value, after authentication success)\n7. **Principal Extraction**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:principal()` (lines 310–314) — builds `SaslAuthenticationContext`; calls `principalBuilder.build(context)`\n8. **Sink**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:build()` (line 84) — `new KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, saslServer.getAuthorizationID())` — client-supplied username becomes the authoritative principal name used for all subsequent ACL checks\n\n### Flow 2: SCRAM Authentication — multi-round challenge to stored-key verification\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — raw bytes from socket, two rounds\n2. **Round 1 Parse**: `ScramMessages.ClientFirstMessage.<init>(byte[])` (lines 82–92) — regex parses client-first message; extracts `saslName` (username, URL-encoded), `nonce`, `authorizationId`, and `extensions`\n3. **Username Decode**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (lines 108–109) — `ScramFormatter.username(saslName)` decodes `=2C`/`=3D` escapes; `NameCallback` set with decoded username\n4. **Credential Lookup**: `ScramServerCallbackHandler.java:handle()` (lines 65–68) — `credentialCache.get(username)` looks up `ScramCredential` by client-supplied username; if absent, authentication fails with generic error\n5. **Server Challenge**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (lines 135–140) — server combines client nonce + server nonce, returns salt and iteration count; state advances to `RECEIVE_CLIENT_FINAL_MESSAGE`\n6. **Round 2 Parse**: `ScramMessages.ClientFinalMessage.<init>(byte[])` — parses client-final message containing channel-binding, full nonce, and HMAC proof\n7. **Nonce Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java` (line 150–151) — `clientFinalMessage.nonce()` must equal server-generated nonce; prevents replay\n8. **Proof Verification**: `ScramSaslServer.java:verifyClientProof()` (lines 229–233) — `MessageDigest.isEqual(computedStoredKey, expectedStoredKey)` — time-constant comparison\n9. **Sink**: `ScramSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` returns `authorizationId` (client-supplied username, post-authentication) → `KafkaPrincipal`\n\n### Flow 3: OAUTHBEARER Authentication — JWT token to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:evaluateResponse()` (line 95) — raw bytes parsed by `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse`\n2. **Parse**: `OAuthBearerClientInitialResponse.<init>(byte[])` (lines 51–76) — regex extracts `authzid`, key-value extension pairs, and the `auth=Bearer <token>` string; `tokenValue` is the raw JWT string\n3. **Validation**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:process()` (lines 154–160) — `OAuthBearerValidatorCallback` constructed with client-supplied JWT string; `callbackHandler.handle()` invoked — validation quality depends entirely on which handler is configured\n4. **Default Handler Gap**: `SaslChannelBuilder.java` (line 331) — if no custom handler configured, `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is used, which accepts **unsigned** JWTs without signature verification\n5. **AuthzId Check**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java` (line 171–173) — if client provides `authorizationId`, it must match `token.principalName()`; if empty, `token.principalName()` is used directly\n6. **Extensions**: `processExtensions()` validates extension key-value pairs; malformed extensions rejected\n7. **Sink**: `OAuthBearerSaslServer.java:getAuthorizationID()` (line 108) — returns `tokenForNegotiatedProperty.principalName()` from the JWT claims → `KafkaPrincipal`\n\n### Flow 4: GSSAPI Kerberos Authentication — GSS tokens to principal\n\n1. **Source**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:authenticate()` — raw Kerberos AP-REQ/AP-REP tokens from client\n2. **GSSAPI Processing**: `SaslServerAuthenticator.java:createSaslKerberosServer()` — `Sasl.createSaslServer()` creates JVM GSSAPI `SaslServer`; GSS token evaluation performed by the JVM's Kerberos implementation\n3. **Callback**: `SaslServerCallbackHandler.java:handleAuthorizeCallback()` (lines 67–73) — **unconditionally** sets `ac.setAuthorized(true)` and `ac.setAuthorizedID(authenticationID)`; no additional authorization checks performed here\n4. **Short-naming**: `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.java:applyKerberosShortNamer()` — applies configured `sasl.kerberos.principal.to.local` rules to transform `user@REALM` to short name\n5. **Sink**: `new KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, shortName)` → ACL matching uses the short name\n\n### Flow 5: Principal to ACL Authorization\n\n1. **Post-authentication**: `KafkaChannel` marks channel as authenticated; `KafkaPrincipal` stored\n2. **Per-request authorization**: Broker request handler calls `Authorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)` per incoming Kafka API request\n3. **ACL lookup**: `StandardAuthorizerData` matches `requestContext.principal()` (the `KafkaPrincipal`) against stored ACL bindings by `principalType:name`, with wildcard `User:*` support\n4. **Super-user bypass**: If `principal.getName()` appears in the configured `super.users` list, all ACL checks are bypassed\n5. **Result**: `ALLOWED` or `DENIED` returned per action; denied requests receive error responses\n\n---\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n```\n[TCP Socket]\n    ↓ raw bytes\nNetworkReceive (size-prefixed, max bounded by saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize)\n    ↓\nSaslServerAuthenticator.authenticate()  [SaslState machine]\n    ↓ INITIAL_REQUEST\nhandleKafkaRequest()\n    ↓ ApiVersionsRequest\n        → clientSoftwareName/Version → ChannelMetadataRegistry  [log injection risk]\n    ↓ SaslHandshakeRequest\n        → clientMechanism validated against enabledMechanisms\n        → createSaslServer(mechanism)\n            ├─ PLAIN  → PlainSaslServer + PlainServerCallbackHandler\n            ├─ SCRAM  → ScramSaslServer + ScramServerCallbackHandler → CredentialCache\n            ├─ OAUTHBEARER → OAuthBearerSaslServer + {OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler | OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler}\n            └─ GSSAPI → JVM GSSAPI SaslServer + SaslServerCallbackHandler\n    ↓ AUTHENTICATE\nhandleSaslToken(clientToken)\n    → SaslServer.evaluateResponse(clientToken)\n        [per-mechanism parsing, validation, credential lookup]\n    → saslServer.isComplete() == true\n    ↓ COMPLETE\nSaslServerAuthenticator.principal()\n    → SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()  [client-controlled value, authenticated]\n    → DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder.build()\n    → KafkaPrincipal(USER_TYPE, authorizationId)\n        ↓\nAuthorizer.authorize(requestContext, actions)\n    → StandardAuthorizerData ACL matching on KafkaPrincipal\n    → AuthorizationResult.ALLOWED / DENIED\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### 1. Insecure Default OAUTHBEARER Validator (Authentication Bypass)\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Authentication bypass / missing cryptographic verification\n\n**Description**: In `SaslChannelBuilder.createServerCallbackHandlers()` (line 331), when the OAUTHBEARER mechanism is enabled without an explicit `sasl.server.callback.handler.class`, the default handler assigned is `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler`. This handler is documented as \"unsecured\" and intended for development/testing only — it accepts self-signed JWTs without verifying the cryptographic signature or checking the issuer/audience claims.\n\n**Impact**: Any attacker can craft a JWT with an arbitrary `sub` (principal name) claim, sign it with any key (or not at all), and authenticate as any user — including super-users. This enables complete ACL bypass if super-users are OAUTHBEARER-authenticated, or impersonation of legitimate users.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The class is named \"Unsecured\" and documentation warns against production use. Operators must explicitly configure `OAuthBearerValidatorCallbackHandler`.\n\n**Gap**: There is no runtime check preventing this insecure handler from being used in production deployments. No warning log is emitted at startup when OAUTHBEARER is enabled with the unsecured handler.\n\n**Attack scenario**:\n1. Broker has `OAUTHBEARER` in `sasl.enabled.mechanisms` with no custom handler configured\n2. Attacker crafts JWT: `{\"alg\":\"none\"}.{\"sub\":\"admin\"}.{}`\n3. Attacker authenticates as `User:admin`; if `super.users=User:admin` is configured, full broker access is granted\n\n**Remediation**: Emit a warning (or fail-fast) at broker startup when OAUTHBEARER is enabled and `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` is active. Consider removing it from the default selection path entirely.\n\n---\n\n### 2. Client-Controlled Principal Name (Trust Boundary Issue)\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Improper input validation / trust boundary violation\n\n**Description**: Across all non-GSSAPI mechanisms, the `authorizationId` that becomes the `KafkaPrincipal` name is derived entirely from client-supplied data:\n- **PLAIN**: `authorizationId = username` from the NUL-delimited payload (`PlainSaslServer.java:110`)\n- **SCRAM**: `authorizationId = username` decoded from `saslName` in the client-first message (`ScramSaslServer.java:123`)\n- **OAUTHBEARER**: `authorizationId = token.principalName()` from the JWT's `sub` claim (which is client-supplied, though validated if signature verification is correctly configured)\n\n**Impact**: While credential verification does gate access, the exact string used for ACL matching is what the client asserts — subject only to the credential existing in the store. A user who knows a valid credential can set their principal name to any case variant, Unicode normalization form, or special-character form that might match a different ACL entry or super-user listing. String comparison in ACL matching is exact (Java `String.equals()`), so this is mitigated by default, but custom `KafkaPrincipalBuilder` implementations could mishandle these inputs.\n\n**PLAIN-specific risk**: `PlainServerCallbackHandler.authenticate()` constructs the JAAS key as `JAAS_USER_PREFIX + username` = `\"user_\" + username`. The username comes directly from the client. A username containing newlines, equals signs, or other JAAS-special characters (e.g., `alice\\nuser_bob=secret`) could potentially interfere with JAAS config parsing in unusual implementations, though the standard `AppConfigurationEntry` map lookup mitigates most cases.\n\n**Existing mitigations**: Credential verification ensures the client must possess the correct credential for the claimed identity. `Utils.isEqualConstantTime()` prevents timing-based username enumeration for PLAIN.\n\n**Gap**: There is no normalization or sanitization of usernames before they are stored as the principal name. Usernames with special characters that are valid from the credential store's perspective could create unexpected ACL-matching behavior.\n\n---\n\n### 3. No Transport-Level Enforcement for PLAIN Mechanism\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Cleartext credential transmission\n\n**Description**: `PLAIN` mechanism transmits credentials (username + password) as unencrypted bytes within the SASL exchange. There is no code-level enforcement preventing `PLAIN` from being used over a `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listener (unencrypted TCP). The broker configuration allows combining `SASL_PLAINTEXT` protocol with `PLAIN` mechanism, resulting in credentials transmitted in cleartext over the network.\n\n`PlainSaslServer.evaluateResponse()` operates identically regardless of whether the underlying transport is TLS-protected (`SASL_SSL`) or plaintext (`SASL_PLAINTEXT`).\n\n**Impact**: Network-level passive eavesdropping yields plaintext credentials, enabling account takeover.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: Documentation recommends `SASL_SSL` for PLAIN. The `SslClientAuth` configuration is set to `NONE` for SASL_SSL listeners (line 153 in ChannelBuilders.java) since SASL takes over authentication.\n\n**Gap**: No runtime warning is emitted when `PLAIN` is configured on `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners; no enforced policy prevents this combination.\n\n---\n\n### 4. GSSAPI: Unconditional Authorization in SaslServerCallbackHandler\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Missing authorization check\n\n**Description**: `SaslServerCallbackHandler.handleAuthorizeCallback()` (lines 67–73) unconditionally sets `ac.setAuthorized(true)` and sets `authorizedID = authenticationID` without any validation logic. This means any principal that successfully completes Kerberos mutual authentication is unconditionally authorized at the SASL layer — the `AuthorizeCallback` mechanism (intended to allow/deny specific authorization IDs) provides no additional protection.\n\n**Impact**: There is no mechanism at the SASL layer to distinguish between Kerberos principals that should and should not be authorized to connect. All validated Kerberos principals pass the SASL authorization check, relying entirely on subsequent Kafka ACL checks.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: Kafka ACL checks downstream provide per-operation authorization. Kerberos mutual authentication ensures the identity is genuine.\n\n**Gap**: The SASL authorization callback is a hook designed to permit, for example, cross-realm or impersonation scenarios to be controlled. Its unconditional implementation removes this control point.\n\n---\n\n### 5. Log Injection via Client-Supplied Software Name and Version\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Log injection / information leakage\n\n**Description**: In `SaslServerAuthenticator.handleApiVersionsRequest()` (lines 581–582), the client-supplied `clientSoftwareName` and `clientSoftwareVersion` strings from the `ApiVersionsRequest` are stored directly in `ChannelMetadataRegistry` without sanitization. These strings are subsequently logged and stored as `ClientInformation`. A malicious client can inject arbitrary strings — including newlines, terminal escape sequences, or structured log patterns — into broker log files.\n\n**Impact**: Log pollution, log injection attacks against log management systems, and potential exploitation of downstream log parsers.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: The `ApiVersionsRequest` schema has string length limits (max 255 characters per field as per the Kafka protocol schema).\n\n**Gap**: Content is not sanitized for control characters or newlines before logging. Log4j/SLF4J patterns do not automatically escape newlines in logged strings.\n\n---\n\n### 6. Mechanism Downgrade — No Protocol-Level Downgrade Protection\n\n**Vulnerability class**: Protocol downgrade attack\n\n**Description**: The `SaslHandshakeRequest` allows the client to select any mechanism from the server's advertised list. The `handleHandshakeRequest()` method (line 550) accepts any `clientMechanism` that is in `enabledMechanisms`. If both `SCRAM-SHA-256` and `PLAIN` are enabled, a man-in-the-middle (operating at the TCP layer, below the Kafka protocol) could modify the handshake to force the client to use `PLAIN`, resulting in credential transmission in cleartext.\n\n**Existing mitigation**: With `SASL_SSL`, the TLS channel protects against MITM at the TCP layer, preventing handshake modification. With `SASL_PLAINTEXT`, no protection exists.\n\n**Gap**: There is no client-side or server-side enforcement of minimum mechanism security requirements, and enabling insecure mechanisms alongside secure ones creates a downgrade surface.\n\n---\n\n### 7. Re-authentication State Machine — Partial Protection\n\n**Description**: The re-authentication flow in `SaslServerAuthenticator.reauthenticate()` correctly calls `reauthInfo.ensurePrincipalUnchanged(principal())` to prevent principal swapping during re-authentication. However, the mechanism must also remain unchanged (`saslMechanismUnchanged()` check at line 532), which prevents mechanism downgrade during re-auth.\n\n**Gap**: The state machine transitions to `REAUTH_BAD_MECHANISM` before updating failure metrics, introducing a window where the failed re-authentication counter is not incremented immediately. This does not constitute a security vulnerability but reduces observability.\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nThe Kafka SASL authentication subsystem implements a well-structured state machine (`SaslServerAuthenticator`) that gates channel access through a sequence of `ApiVersionsRequest` → `SaslHandshakeRequest` → mechanism-specific token exchange before accepting any Kafka protocol requests. Principal identity flows from `SaslServer.getAuthorizationID()` (set by each mechanism implementation from client-supplied, credential-verified data) through `DefaultKafkaPrincipalBuilder` into `KafkaPrincipal`, which is then used for all downstream ACL checks by `Authorizer.authorize()`.\n\n**The most significant security finding** is that the OAUTHBEARER mechanism defaults to `OAuthBearerUnsecuredValidatorCallbackHandler` when no custom handler is configured — an implementation that accepts unsigned JWTs without cryptographic verification. In a production environment where this handler is active, any attacker can authenticate as an arbitrary user (including super-users), achieving complete Kafka cluster compromise. This is an authentication bypass of the highest severity.\n\nSecondary findings include: PLAIN mechanism carrying credentials in cleartext over `SASL_PLAINTEXT` listeners; the GSSAPI `AuthorizeCallback` being unconditionally approved (eliminating a potential control point); client-supplied software name/version strings being logged without sanitization (log injection); and the absence of downgrade protection when multiple mechanisms of differing security strength are enabled simultaneously.\n\nMitigations that **do work correctly** include: constant-time password comparison in PLAIN (`Utils.isEqualConstantTime`); HMAC-based proof verification in SCRAM using `MessageDigest.isEqual` (time-constant); configurable receive size limits (`saslAuthRequestMaxReceiveSize`) preventing buffer exhaustion during authentication; principal-change enforcement during re-authentication; and nonce verification in SCRAM preventing replay attacks.\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}
